There are so many reasons I shouldn‚Äôt love this book. The characters often feel distant. The prose style, while technically competent, can be stuffy and is not always engaging. There are long stretches where it feels like the plot is moving forward at a snail‚Äôs pace. Made-up words are hurled at the reader at every turn. And, considering the 1965 publication date, there are certain elements like gender dynamics which simply didn‚Äôt age well and had me rolling my eyes from time to time.

And yet.

For whatever reason (or combination of reasons), I love this book. Love it wholeheartedly and absolutely. There is something that just feels so brilliant, so awe-inspiring, that instilled in me a sense of wonder I haven‚Äôt felt in years. Worldbuilding has always come secondary to me, as I prefer to focus more on characters and story, but this is the first time I can recall an actual being so impressive to me.

I became obsessed with . When I wasn‚Äôt reading it, I was thinking about it, daydreaming about the harsh world of Arrakis. Even though I‚Äôd already purchased the ebook version and was 60% through, I still spent another $30 on the beautiful deluxe hardcover, and began rereading it from the beginning.

Why? Because I could tell this novel was something special for me. I knew it was something I wanted to cherish in my physical book collection, something I could perhaps revisit.

I am so happy to have discovered this wondrous world. Despite the warnings of many trusted GR friends, I plan to dive headfirst into the rest of the series. I just can‚Äôt help myself.

So what is it that made me love this so much? I‚Äôm really not sure. It could be the age-old feeling of epic storytelling, filled with prophecy and heroic archetypes. It could be the underlying philosophy and thematic resonance, layers upon layers that touch upon things like politics, economics, ecology, and more. And despite not really liking many of the characters (including Paul, who I'm not convinced we are even to like), there were some who I really grew to care about... Jessica, Chani, Gurney, Hawat, and even Alia**! And particularly near the end of the book, there were a few emotionally-stirring moments.

Or maybe it was the sandworms.

positive
~~~
I guess I'm one of the few that bridge the gap between the Pride and Prejudice camp and the Dune camp. I loved both.

Dune isn't a light, enjoyable read. At times it reads more like excerpts from geology, ecology, zoology, sociology, pscyhology, and political textbooks. The characters are more like mega-archetypes than real human beings.

The appeal of Dune is peculiar. In order to enjoy Dune you have to enjoy complexity. All authors create little worlds in their stories but Herbert created a .

He doesn't just say that Arrakis is a desert planet, he engrosses himself and the reader into the geology.

He puts people on the planet, governments, conflicting cultures, conflicting religions, conflicting ways of life that are thought out to the Nth level above and beyond anything else I've ever read. You could write a sociology or politics dissertation on the societal relations Herbert conceived for Dune.

Now is complexity itself a thing to be admired in a work of fiction? Generally no, but Dune is so immense and so detailed that it creates and inhabits a category of its own. The very fact that it often reads more like a National Geographic article than a sci-fi novel speaks to its peculiar charm.

Admittedly, this will not appeal to everyone. In fact, odds are that it will appeal to hardly anyone. But limited appeal should in no way factor into a work's quality. Compare the Academy Award-winning films against the yearly box office numbers if you don't agree. I'm sure Armaggeddon outgrossed Monster's Ball.

And amidst all this complexity lies a kind of new myth that blends mysticism, religion, and crass real-world politics. It's a hybrid; it's not The Odyssey and it's certainly not Star Wars but I do find great appeal in its particular take on Campbell's hero's journey. And the fact that it plumbs the intricacies of Muslim/Arab/desert culture adds another layer of exotic flair to the work.

As if all that wasn't ambitious enough, it even articulates a fascinatingly dark but pragmatic destiny for humanity as a whole.

And all of these incredibly ambitious elements are all tightly woven together. Take out one element and the story loses its cohesion. Despite all the ridiculous amounts of detail there is nothing extraneous in this novel.

Dune is a remarkable, magnificent accomplishment. But it's okay if it's not to your taste.

~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

~~~
positive
~~~
Dune came out in 1965, but it might have been yesterday. Indeed, this book just doesn‚Äôt seem to age at all! This is mainly due to the fact that by eradicating all form of computerized technology from his story, Frank Herbert managed with great talent to render it timeless while simultaneously anchoring it to the foundation of the Science Fiction genre. So many SF books written in the 60‚Äôs, 70‚Äôs, 80‚Äôs, and 90‚Äôs come across as dated simply because the pseudo-technologies that then were amazing to the readers now seem downright ridiculous and even laughable. Not so with Dune. Dune is here to stay. Add to this an amazing world-building, well-crafted characters and unforgettable villains, a twisting plot tackling among other things some still-very-up-to-date-and-even-more-so-nowadays environmental issues, deep-but-very-accessible gems of philosophy dispatched here and there within the text and at the beginning of each chapter, and you have here one of the most successful and influential piece of Science Fiction literature of all time. And deservedly so.

OLIVIER DELAYE
Author of the SEBASTEN OF ATLANTIS series

Filled with politics and religion, and the politics of religion, Dune follows House Atreides' new rule over the spice-drug producing planet of Arrakis.

Whispers begin to spread of the legend of an off-world prophet that was long ago chiseled into the minds of a people. And the people have a mind of their own.

~~~
positive
~~~
High up in the clouds ringing the Science Fiction Olympus, at the very peak of the mountain of the writing gods, sits Frank Herbert, seated on a throne of crisp, first-edition copies of Dune, a golden, sandworm-shaped staff in his right hand.

Herbert is an SF deity, and his elevation to godhood comes from one book, and one book alone. Sure he wrote a bunch of other novels, several of which continue the story started in , and several of which are interesting reads. None of them, however, equalled the impact of his most famous work, and the book his name has become synonymous with.

is central to science fiction. You can't call yourself an SF fan if you haven't read it, or at least attempted to do so.

This is an epic novel, one of the true classics of the genre along with books like , . , and .

Herbert introduces readers to an interstellar empire founded on Melange - also known as Spice - an addictive substance that profoundly alters its users, allowing them to fold spacetime and travel vast distances instantaneously, a substance found only on the planet Arrakis. He shows us two noble families competing for the favour of their emperor and the control of the harvesting of this substance. In the great deserts of Arrakis he brings us to gigantic sandworms that hunt via the vibrations of footsteps. Finally we meet the Fremen of the arid wastes who survive wearing stillsuits that recycle their sweat, their urine, even the moisture they exhale.

All of this exists in a deep and convincing future that is utterly computer-less, the result of an AI-led war centuries prior that has seen thinking machines of any type destroyed without mercy wherever they are found. In this future the work of AIs is done by hyper-enhanced and trained humans called mentats, who serve where computers once did.

Into this strange world comes Paul Atreides, a young heir to a noble house, betrayed and marooned in the desert, but destined to change entirely the universe in which he has grown up. His story is a big one, and it will stick with you.

It's all heady, imagination-firing stuff, and just thinking about it thrills me with the limitless potential of the SF genre.

's influence is incalculable. references to it turn up in every medium, from the intelligence enhancing 'mentats' tablets in the games, through to worm sign in and a whole load of setting and characters in . It looms in the subconscious of anyone writing in this genre, whether they are aware of it or not.

The reputation of this book is well earned. It‚Äôs a damn fine story, and an astonishing feat of imagination. Read it. You‚Äôll probably love it, and even if you don‚Äôt you‚Äôll learn a lot about SF and the major influences in the genre.

neutral

3.5 stars

"I must not fear. Fear is the mind-killer."

Another buddy read with the awesomest of friends, TS, Emma and Celeste, is done!

Turns out we need not have rushed to read this one before the movie, but in hindsight, this writing was on the wall. As for the story, I went into this not expecting to enjoy it much, and I was pleasantly surprised, at least for a while.

Dune is of course, much loved and very well known as a staple of the science fiction genre and reading this for the first time I can very much see why. The story turned about to be a bit of a page turner, up to a point, filled with themes of power, control, religion, politics and family, all wrapped up in a intriguing promised one type plot with some excellent world-building and fascinating characters. And while I can easily say that Dune has stood the test of time and I am happy to have read it, my excitement for the story got lost a bit in all the philosophy and the weirder moments in the book, and lastly the ending which left quite a bit to be desired. All in all a very enjoyable read though.

~~~
positive
~~~
There's so much great science fiction writing out there now that you forget that it used to be a looked down upon stepchild of "serious literature". Frank Herbert was one of the authors who caused this evolution of science fiction from "the natural child of someone" to great art in and of itself.

A great story well told, Dune has a few tiny discomforts for the modern reader; such as the slight misogyny of the sisterhood of the Bene Gesserit witches and Paul's contemptuous treatment of his mother in the desert. One of the signs to look for in any work for the treatment of women is are they concerned with more than just their sons and husbands and do they have power.

In this, Herbert is guilty of the first, Lady Jessica, not even the wife of her royal husband, but his concubine, is mostly concerned with son and husband. For the second part though, she does have immense power and can use the power of her voice to control men. She and Paul, her son and future leader, take an impromptu trip through the harsh desert and come upon the fremen who have made a rich life in the barren terrain. This voyage is one of the strongest parts of the story and shows Lady Jessica as a powerful figure herself. The dynamic push and pull between mother and son prefigures the trap that Paul may fall into as he becomes a messianic leader.

~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

~~~
neutral
~~~
3ish stars.

Impressive, epic, creative, revolutionary, classic, not for me. The sort of politics-heavy, languidly-paced, intellectually-stimulating beast that my mind respects more than my emotions connect with.

I don't know what it is about this book in particular that I never clicked with because I've loved other dense, cerebral, worldbuilding sci-fi books that are similar thematically, and this is sort of the granddaddy of them all. I think part of it is that I never became fully invested in the characters. I also felt like I was working too hard for it to be the transportive, escapist masterpiece I was expecting.

During much of the middle I would frequently find myself thinking "I'd rather just watch the movie condensed to only the best bits than spend so much time here." I realize that's blasphemy to most book enthusiasts, and fans of this book in particular. Whoops. But let me tell you, I am about the new movie adaptation coming out later this year! I think director Denis Villneuve (Arrival, Blade Runner 2049) is a so providing the screenwriters (including Eric Roth of Forrest Gump and Benjamin Button) get it right and it's edited tightly, it has the potential to be the Dune I've been hoping for.

positive

I had no problem with Chani romance this time, there‚Äôs something precious and beautiful in a love story that has no start, making it a love without borders of time. And no, I haven‚Äôt seen the movie.



This book is an ancient greek epic written by Homer but in space.

First few hundred pages are a bit dense and a lot of people I know stopped reading in the beginning. But if you keep going, you‚Äôll find a unique and epic world. I see where Stargate, Star Wars, Avatar and even Tremors got inspirations. A lot of politics, religion and even magic.

It‚Äôs a plot or maybe even idea driven story. Characters are not very complex. And I don‚Äôt know if I caught everything on the first reading. We have a lot of inner monologues and all big battles happened behind the scenes.

But I loved Arrakis. And I want to see it on a big screen.

positive
~~~
I did buy the new and beautifull edition celebrating its original publishing in 1965 and expected movie this year, which was the one movie I was really looking forward this year and with Covid-19 being the factor that stopped this party I decided to reread this novel once more.

As a fifteen old kid who kept annoying the ladies of my local library with my great thirst for reading books they had decided to offer me some books to keep me busy. First of was Lord of the Rings by one Tolkien which got devoured with a speed they considered somewhat unsettling they then gave me this book Dune a scifi book. With my scifi taste being mostly Star Trek and Star Wars this book was of course an eye opener and something of new territory for a young kid. For I while I read quite a lot of scifi, classics and whatnot but Dune and its sequels always remained special.
I did read the three sequels, Messiah, Children and God Emperor and was and am still today totally in awe of these books. When I discovered the next two Frank Herbert books I put one on my reading list for English and my teacher who turned out to be a fan too disputed that books existence. Until I put it on his desk and he decided that afternoon to visit the bookstore to obtain a copy and during my oral exam we did discuss this book and I am sure I learned a lot of insight from this English teacher more than he learned from his student. But this teacher loved students that read and came up with different choices than the average student who read because they must.

Anyhow this phenomenal Scifi book tells a story about a duke and his family whom get displaced from their own watery world to a desert world where the article Spice is being found and its application is considered to be a boon to health, prescience and is actually very important in Spaceflight by the Guild navigators. It is difficult to not slip into information later learned in the sequels Messiah & Children because they feel actually not like book 2 & 3 but part of the original tale of Dune.
Paul Atreides loses his father to the treacherous Harkonnen on the planet Arrakis also know as Dune. He becomes the leader of the desert people that inhabit the planet and leads them on a path to religious Jihad and throw the other houses of the Landsraad and the Emperor aside.
This sounds like a lot of action but most of the story is weaving a new world and its secrets and culture. There is so much to be enjoyed in Frank Herberts creation and often you are left in some bewilderment how one man could make up so much different cultures of humanity and there is not an alien around.
My advise would be to read the three books that make up the story of the Atreides and the genetic line they represent and the duty they have for the Universe.
Recognize the prescience that leaves Paul Atreides some choices and he is definitely afraid of his choices and the future he will create. This story tells about a special young man who is meant to change the universe and yet is terribly afraid for the future he will create and yet must do so or die and leave the universe in danger for the future.
The story represents some Greek tragedy which admittingly was also some of Frank Herberts source/inspiration, the foreword by Brian Herbert who went on to enlarge his fathers Duniverse does give you some insight in his fathers writing and thinking.
For me the Bene Gesserit have always been the most fascinating of creations and considering their role in the Duniverse they were important too for Frank Herbert. It is their part in the story that gets everything rolling.

This particular copy is absolutely beautiful and one might hope that the next two installments also get the same treatment with a new release as there is plenty of time and need before we get to see the movie next year. They actually deserve the same respect as they are one story and it would look amazing on my bookshelves of course.

An obvious inspiration for so many to come, with a fascinating fictional science. The author takes his time for everything, and because of that, the ending is brilliant. You got to be patient, Dune delivers.

"Dune" is a cultural keystone, so I had to read it. But I don't have to like it.

1 star. I do not care if it can be read as a futuristic reimagining of the Book of Exodus, or if it informed the zeitgeist of early Nerd Culture. This book is boring. Even the action scenes are boring. Even the cool and interesting parts of the setting are boring. Frank Herbert sure could imagine a detailed, internally consistent, unique and symbolic world, and having read his work will certainly help me answer a few questions at trivia night. But he can't hold my attention for beans.

Ok, so, this was good, but it wasn‚Äôt amazing necessarily looking at it from a more modern lens, but I certainly respect it a lot for what it has done for the genre. My rating is really 3.5 stars, it‚Äôs just above a three, but also not quite a four, but ya know, Goodreads doesn‚Äôt have half stars, so I‚Äôve just rounded it up to a four. Just as a note, I haven‚Äôt read a huge number of sci-fi novels, especially more classic sci-fi, like this novel is, so I don‚Äôt have a lot of older works to compare my review of Dune with, and everything is just my opinion here, as reviews as subjective.

For the characters, I found them to be alright. They weren‚Äôt the most amazing, but nor were they horrible and there were certainly some really nice moments between them. Compared to some of the stuff by more modern authors, I would have preferred for them to have stronger voices and more to separate them between each other. Some of the characters felt like they were sort of just there to advance the plot, or have the character go in a certain direction. I was sort of surprised at how much of the book, especially in the first half that spent a lot of time following Jessica, but I was fine with that as she was an interesting character, though she did become a lot less relevant in the third part. Paul was fine, he‚Äôs essentially the chosen one and it played out in an interesting, though not amazing way.

The world was really nice, as I expected. One thing about Dune that I have heard is at how groundbreaking the world was for its time and what it did for sci-fi, much like LOTR did for fantasy. I certainly agree with that and especially for its time, would have been amazing. There definitely was a lot of depth to the world, and it was a pretty complex, with a chunky appendix at the end that really built up it well. The book is largely spent on the planet of Arrakis, which was interesting in how the world worked and functioned, and the Fremen were fascinating to read about. In my view, the world was probably the strongest element of the world for me. Compared to some of the stuff that I‚Äôve read in the fantasy genre(I know this is sci-fi), it doesn‚Äôt stand out as much, though it certainly is still good. There were also some more fantastical elements that I wasn‚Äôt aware was in it before I started reading it, as I initially though it was hard sci-fi, when in reality, it is more sci-fantasy, which was cool.

I don‚Äôt know if I would say if I really vibe with the writing style. For the most part it was fine, though there were a few things that sort of pulled me out from being immersed all the time. I don‚Äôt know if I liked at how, while yes, it‚Äôs told in third person, the actual point of view kept switching every few paragraphs sometimes so that we followed the thought process of different people and that was just jarring to me. There was also the whole a-h-h-h, or wel-l-l-l in dialogue, that sort of bothered me because you just never see it done anymore and it‚Äôs less a critique of his writing, but rather just that I could not get accustomed to it. There were also lots of character thought written all in italics, and for some reason that managed to take me out of being fully immersed in the text.

The plot was decent, if not somewhat predictable in many of the events. We do spend some time following the antagonists in the story, so that gives us a bit of view of sort of where the plot is going. I would like to think that for its time, where plots weren‚Äôt always super strong, it would have been really good for sure. I enjoyed the ending of this and clearly leaves a lot of stuff for the story to move ahead in for the rest of the series. It wasn‚Äôt brilliant of an ending, but it was still good, if that makes sense. I honestly at this point don‚Äôt know if I will continue with the series, as I have heard mixed things about the sequels. Either way, this book wrapped up nicely by itself, so I don‚Äôt really have too much of an urge to read on.

One area in the plot that I didn‚Äôt love was the significant time jump between Parts 2 and 3, where I think two-three years pass in a blink of an eye. The author also doesn‚Äôt tell you this and you do have to figure it yourself, which look, was fine, I had no problem with that. My main issue with the time jump is that a fair number of significant events occur during that time frame, including some important things for the main characters. Now, when we hear of these things or when events occur, as the reader, I didn‚Äôt really feel any sort of connection to some events in Part 3 that were important to the characters, but I just couldn‚Äôt care much about it. Maybe smaller and gradual time jumps would have helped, considering this occurs two thirds through the book as the sudden jump was jarring. I‚Äôm perfectly fine with time jumps at the start of books, or between books, but near the end was sort of just weird for me personally. I have seen this in Feist‚Äôs , so maybe it was more common in classic works, I don‚Äôt know.

I would say that it has aged fairly well, mostly because of the really solid worldbuilding that holds up really well and that enough time is spent making the main characters to be somewhat interesting. It also does draw on other cultures and in not just centred on Western cultures which, sadly, wasn‚Äôt done back in the 1960s. This has changed greatly now, for which I am super glad about with way more BIPOC authors being published, which is fantastic. I would imagine that in the Western world at least, this would have been revolutionary.

So overall, I thought that this was good, but I suppose it failed to blow me away. I respect it for what it has achieved and how much of an influence it has had on the sci-fi genre, but respect does not always equal enjoyment or love for something. 7/10

I know a lot of people love this book and I can understand why especially after reading the reviews. I personally didn't enjoy it very much though. It felt like it took me at least 300 pages to really get into the story. The book felt very long and I felt like I had to keep redirecting my attention back to reading because I wasn't feeling engaged. Part of it might be because it took me a while to get used to the new words being thrown around and at first I had trouble keep track of the characters. Also the way the Fremen were clearly inspired by Bedouins and Islam just kept distracting me. I did start enjoying it much more once I got halfway through it, probably because the action felt like it picked up. I don't think I'll be reading the other books in the series though.

~~~
positive
~~~
Dune is a beautiful and complex fiction masterpiece. Frank Herbert built up an incredibly vivid world, layer upon layer, with multiple religious/belief systems, strongly developed cultures which clashed with each other and a thousand currents of political manoeuvring, from the lowest classes up to the emperor himself.

It took a very long time for this novel to reach any action, but I didn‚Äôt mind one bit, because I enjoyed really getting to know each character and exploring their universe. I found there wasn‚Äôt a single character I wasn‚Äôt invested in, or at least interested in. Paul, Jessica and Alia were particularly my favourites, especially with their Bene Gesserit insights/training. I must say, I really, thoroughly enjoyed the clever use of the giant desert worms in this novel.

My only disappointment was the ending, not the way it ended, but the way it was told. I felt it was too rushed, there was too much telling and not enough showing, and the scene unfolding cut off before completion. I would have liked to see all the threads neatly tied up, but I guess these will be picked up in the next book.

A few weeks ago when I was trying to decide which book I should read next and starring at my book shelf I set my eyes upon a thick book, with a dark cover. The title read ‚ÄúDUNE‚Äù and above that was written ‚ÄúScience Fiction‚Äôs Supreme Masterpiece‚Äù. Dune had been on my shelf for, I don‚Äôt know, maybe five or six years and I had always lacked the motivation to actually read it whenever I thought of its length. But this time something called my attention, I don‚Äôt know exactly what, maybe it was the golden colour of the sand drawn on the cover or maybe it was the crease on the tip of the cover.

I opened the book and began to read the first page. There was a long quote from the ‚ÄúManual of Muad‚ÄôDib‚Äù on the first half of the page, apparently written ‚Äúby the Princess Irulan‚Äù, and there were words I didn‚Äôt recognise, like Bene Gesserit, Muad‚ÄôDib, Caladan and Arrakis. I kept on reading. A few pages later, as time flew by, I was still reading and turning the pages. And suddenly I reached the end of the first chapter. I just wanted to keep on reading and I was suddenly completely drawn into this book which I had avoided for so long. In the first Chapter I had only met Paul Atreides, Jessica and the Reverend Mother Gaius Helen Mohiam. There was also a mysterious test that Paul was going to face, which involved an object named ‚Äúgom jabbar‚Äù.

In the following chapters I met the Duke Leto Atreides, Paul‚Äôs father, and some members from his household, including Gurney Halleck, Duncan Idaho, Dr. Yueh and the ‚ÄúMentat‚Äù Thufir Hawat. I learned that the Atreides family was in a dynastic feud with the Harkonnens and the plot was full of intrigue, betrayal and deception. The members of the Atreides faction were worried and concerned about moving from their home planet of Caladan to the desert planet of Arrakis, also known as Dune, where water might be the most valuable possession one could have, even more so than gold, where there are sandworms and also spice - ‚Äúthe crop for which Arrakis in the unique source‚Äù.

After years of delaying the day I would read Dune, I just couldn‚Äôt put it down, even after what some might consider to be a somewhat slow start to the story, which gets deeper as the plot advances and we learn more about Dune‚Äôs cultural, religious, political, sociological and ecological landscape. In fact, there are far too many terms in Dune which are unfamiliar to the reader at first, but I later discovered that Dune‚Äôs complexity, as well as its length, lead to its depth and I found myself becoming emotionally attached to characters which felt genuine.

I could go on to mention all the historical, cultural and religious themes which influenced Frank Herbert‚Äôs writing. I could mention how much some parts of the plot reminded me of Arabic culture and traditions. Or how surprising it was to see ecology being heavily featured in a science fiction book. But I will just say that I‚Äôm glad I opened Dune after all this years and read it. Dune is a truly epic story and a classic of science fiction.

positive
~~~
DBR to follow, at Ceridwen's request. She must have used one of her Bene Gesserit tricks on me.

***
Dune. Dune is a fascinating book. A classic of science-fiction, it plays equally as fantasy and allegory. It is deeply textured, richly layered. And if you want to read a sensible review of it, I'd go read, say, Cedriwen's. This one is going to be full of silliness.

Arrakis. The desert planet. Home to spice and sandworms. Dune. You know, I went to the desert once. The Sahara Desert. It looked like this (ps I took this picture!):



I don't feel like tracking down a picture from the movie, so you have to imagine what Arrakis looks like. It looks a lot like the Sahara.

So anyway, one of the "things to do" when you're in Morocco is to drive out into the Sahara and go for a camel ride, stay overnight in a Berber tent. Ah, camels. I don't know if any of you have ridden a camel (but I'm sure if you have, you will back me up on this): not as much fun as you would think. Riding a horse is, let's face it, always a little uncomfortable. You have to straddle it (twss!), you have to keep your legs at a weird angle (twss!), it starts to hurt after a while (uh... twss?). Riding a camel, on the other hand, sucks. Camels do not wear saddles. They wear blankets. A camel's hump is not very squishy for something that is supposed to be full of water, if my elementary school understanding of why they can live in the desert is accurate. If kind of feels like you are sitting on a rock, and it feels like someone is repeatedly hitting your public bone with a baseball bat. This is not just a guy thing; my girlfriend confirmed that after about five minutes, riding a camel ceases being at all fun and becomes an exercise in pain management. It hurt like hell while it was happening, and it was hard to walk the next day (om, twss!).

On Dune, the desert-inhabiting Fremen don't ride . They ride these:



Scary. But probably not more uncomfortable to ride than a camel.

Look, this is me with Fergie. I am smiling because I am no longer riding it.



Other than the sand and stuff, Arrakis is pretty different from Western Africa. Like the sandworms, which attack anyone who dares venture into the desert, there are the Fremen, a mysterious tribe of fighters who have a strange relationship with what they call "the Markers," there is this stuff called spice that must be carefully harvested from the sands, the risk rewarded by the incredible worth of the spice as a drug used by the Guild to gives the navigators the prescient sight they need to chart courses through hyperspace, and by the Bene Gesserit witches, why use it to preserve their ways from generation to generation. Also I don't think Dune had package tours.

Dune is a famously boring and complicated book. You may have heard that it has a lengthy appendix, and that when David Lynch turned it into a movie, the story was so complex that audiences had to be given cheat sheets along with their tickets. (Don't blame Frank Herbert for that, though. The Dune movie is just marvelously bad, as this chart will illustrate:

)

Don't let its reputation fool you. Sure, Dune is a little bit boring in parts (the characters like to philosophize redundantly and have prophetic visions). But it is also an awesome space opera, with an entirely transporting sense of place and vidiv world-building. The political struggles that drive the plot engine are fiendishly complex to the occasional point of obtusion (I can't believe that is a word), but also a lot of fun to read about, thanks to sneering villains like Baron Harkonnen, who isn't just any old bad guy, but a grossly obese, slithery, murdering, rapist pedophile. The Fremen, a tribe of deeply spiritual religious fanatics who control a natural resource and are willing to die for what they believe... in. And then there are the sandworms. I loved everything about this concept, from Herbert's vivid descriptions, to their presence as an omnipresent threat throughout the book, to their epic inclusion in the explosion and violence-heavy climax. Awesome.

I haven't really said anything about the book. It's good. It's Dune. It is a super-famous classic for a reason. It has compelling characters (Lady Jessica is one of the most interesting female protagonists in classic sci-fi, and the book's take on gender politics, with an elite group of women are a powerful, secretive and controlling force in the galaxy , is worth examining.) It is epic in scope. It's nearly as deep as Tolkien (there's that appendix).

It's also just cool.

~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

positive

Absolutely phenomenal. I regret not reading this sooner but I think this will become a favourite. Maybe something I read each year. There's a lot to take in so I might add more to this review later (especially as I'm now going to read the 5 sequels)

A must read

~~~
negative
~~~
Man oh man, this was just not for me. The dialogue is insanely clunky and stilted. The linear world building, it doesn't feel like a world being given to you to explore but rather a railroad track tour of the world. Woe to the reader if you want to get off the track.

I've seen people come to the defense of this saying well it was written in the 60's in terms of dialogue, info dumps, monologues, and strange terms that aren't well explained. That's not really an excuse, in my opinion. The large issue is that it seems to not have aged well, at all. It's been well surpassed writing wise.

The near constant recaps of the previous chapters are largely unnecessary and just serve to annoy rather than help. The ending...was never really in doubt telegraphed from about 3 miles away with our near perfect great white hope savior. It was just more of a relief for me to finish it than be happy about it.

That said, I can see where/why this novel is the Grandaddy so to speak of the science fiction world. I just think it's time to stop measuring anything newer against it, politely nodding as Grandad rants and raves while we help him walk to his bed and put him to rest. I'm glad I read it in the sense that one is glad they try a new food and learn they don't like it so at least they can say they have the experience of doing so and their opinion is grounded in experience.

~~~
positive
~~~
It's been years since I've read straight up science fiction or fantasy, and this was a wonderful mixture of the two. It was an internally consistent world vividly portrayed, Herbert has a touch of the poetic in describing his desert planet Arrakis, which I appreciated. I listened to it in an audio version that used actors to play the various roles, which at first I found irritating but later settled into and enjoyed immensely.

Dune traces the coming of age of a new King--Paul Atreides-- as the House Atreides is moved from the wet planet Callisto to the desert planet--and we move from a vaguely Greek (House of Atreides) flavor of myth to one with vaguely Arabic overtones on the desert planet. Women surprisingly feature largely in the book--especially those belonging to the secret all-female Bene Gesserrit order, women imbued with great powers and knowledge, to which Paul's mother belongs. It's a basic hero plot but well done, with rival baddie house House Harkonnen, and lots of interesting devices and orders of character. The era's fascination with psychedelic drugs is well-captured by the treasure of Arrakis--Spice, which the competing planetary Houses and the space transport Guild rely upon.

A real Ace/Tor old school sci fi fantasy. I'd gone through an intense period of sci fi and fantasy reading earlier in my life-- lots of Zelazny, Philip K. Dick , Ursula K. LeGuin and Philip Jose Farmer-- but had not tried Dune until the film tickled my interest.

As a writer, it was fascinating to see the things Herbert does exceptionally well. He handles a constantly-shifting omniscient point of view wonderfully well--something you'd never see in a Dick novel--so that all the cards are always on the table, and each scene shimmers with the varying points of view. He's also masterful in building suspense, packing the buildup to each major scene action with detail, much the way Poe did. I enjoyed it tremendously while I was reading it. Five stars for pure escapist entertainment.

I might have fallen in love with Gurney Halleck.

I‚Äôm not typically a fantasy or sci-fi fan, but this one really drew me in. The story is extremely compelling, the type you don‚Äôt want to quit reading, even if it‚Äôs long past midnight.

~~~
positive
~~~
Four and a half sandworms.

I did not expect it, it has been a whole rediscovery. It is my third reading of Dune but from the previous ones many years ago. I did not remember how complete this novel is, as a great adventure, as its own worldbuilding and philosophy, as space opera galactic empire and of course as a sense of wonder: the desert, the worms, the greatness they evoke. Definitely a great classic that has not become outdated since it was published in 1965.

PS: I do not intend to read the sequels, many people tell me that it is not worth it. Apparently for die-hard fans only. So now we have to wait for the Denis Villeneuve's movie.

Top 10 favourite book of all time! I'm having a good run of books from that point of view at the moment.

Told in the voice of an omniscient narrator the plot unfolds with practised ease around the cast. I'm not used to this style of narration and found it to be very powerful. Knowing the private thoughts and reactions of relevant characters in certain scenes was often vastly more revealing than anything else could have been.

The world building was simply brilliant. The universe with it's politics and intrigues grew naturally and elegantly as the book progressed. There were a number of fascinating details such as the outlawing of computers and training humans to be computer-like called Menats. I wonder how hard it would be to train us to be machine like in our thinking? There are other 'abilities' humans develop through training that I wonder about.

Paul is one of my favourite characters ever written and I knew that was going to be true fairly early on.


I can see why this is hailed as a classic and would recommend it to anyone and everyone. Can't wait to get on with the rest of the series.

One of my favourite quotes from the book:

"There is in all things a pattern that is part of our universe. It has symmerty, elegance, and grace -- those qualities you find always in that which the true artist captures. You can find it in the turning of the seasons, in the way sand trails along a ridge, in the branch clusters of the creosote bush or the pattern of its leaves. We try to copy these patterns in our lives and our society, seeking the rhythms, the dances, the forms that comfort. Yet, it is possible to see peril in the finding of untimate perfection. It is clear that the ultimate pattern contains its own fixity. In such perfection, all things move toward death."

This novel is a terrific exercise in world-building, and a fantastic space opera to boot. The desert planet Arrakis (where even body fluids have to be recycled through "stillsuits", so great is the water scarcity) is one of the most enchanting fictional worlds I have ever visited. And the story! - truly mythical in its dimension with empire, intrigue and desert warfare. I loved it all the way through.

With the Lady Jessica and Arrakis, the Bene Gesserit system of sowing implant-legends through the Missionaria Protectiva came to its full fruition ‚Ä¶ And it is generally accepted now that her latent abilities were grossly underestimated
- From ‚ÄúAnalysis: The Arrakeen Crisis‚Äù by the Princess Irulan


2015 was the 50th anniversary of Frank Herbert‚Äôs , which was published by Chilton in 1965.





(Wiki)









The author was known as one of the earliest fiction writers to speak about the ecosystem and ecological concerns. The Afterward by his son is a good source for personal information about Frank Herbert. Another on-line source I happened across if this article from




Dune and its five sequels
constitute the best selling SF series of all time. In the depth of its tale of human rule in the galaxy it has been compared to Tolkien‚Äôs saga. Like Tolkien, Herbert produced elements of a language used in the saga, containing enough special terms that he included a glossary in the book. These terms were derived by Herbert from many different earth languages. The Afterward in the edition I read lists Navajo, Latin, Chakobsa (of the Caucasus), the Nahuatl dialect of the Aztecs, Greek, Persian, East Indian, Russian, Turkish, Finnish, Old English ‚Äì and last, but hardly least, since it stands out particularly, Arabic.


Herbert included a map with the novel (like Tolkien‚Äôs map of Middle Earth) and four appendices, in which he added detail to the novel ‚Äì The Ecology of Dune, The Religion of Dune, Report on Bene Gesserit Motives and Purposes, and The Almanak En-Ashraf (Selected excerpts of the Noble Houses).




Set over 20,000 years in the future, at a time when humans have colonized (from Earth) most of the galaxy. The galaxy-wide governing Imperium is medieval in character, with an Emperor ruling over Great Houses. The novel takes place on the planet Arrakis, a remote location of unimaginable value to both the Emperor and the Great Houses - being the only known source in the galaxy for . This is a spice which is used crucially by the ; to prolong life and protect against disease (by those able to obtain it); and (by the ) to enhance their secret mental abilities.






The , rules the Imperium at the time of the novel. He is the 81st such ruler in his bloodline, the House Corrino. His fighting forces, the Sardaukar, are the most vicious and feared warriors in the galaxy.



The Spacing Guild is a powerful commercial entity having a monopoly on both interstellar travel and banking within the Imperium. Of special note are Guild , who (through a cultivated addiction to melange) have developed the ability to guide spacecraft from one star system to another through ‚Äúfolded space‚Äù at instantaneous speeds.



House Atreides, in the person of (the Red Duke), begins the novel ruling the planet Caladan. Leto is pressured by the Emperor to move his kingdom to Arrakis near the start of the novel, and take over the governance of that important planet. Leto is the father of , the main character of the novel. Paul‚Äôs mother, a concubine of the Duke‚Äôs, is ‚Äì a Bene Gesserit who is the main female character.



House Harkonnen is a house with blood relation to House Atreides, but at the time of the novel the two houses have become sworn enemies. House Harkonnen supplies most of the villains for the story, the chief being .



The Bene Gesserit are an ancient society of women, both well-known in the Imperium but also very secret regarding their mental abilities and ultimate purposes. Often referred to in the novel as ‚Äúwitches‚Äù, by characters who hate and fear them.



The Fremen are the native, desert-dwelling population of Arrakis. They play a momentous role in , and in the sequels.




Herbert was not interested in writing a novel exploring technological advances in the far future. This is one of the things which set his novel apart from most science fiction of the 1960s. Rather, he wished to tell a story about the development of human psychology, mental abilities, and religion, as man explored and colonized the galaxy. Herbert developed an interesting means of taking technology out of the story ‚Äì the Butlerian Jihad. (Information about this is contained in the appendices, not much in the story itself.) The Butlerian Jihad was a war that mankind waged against ‚Äúcomputers, thinking machines and conscious robots‚Äù approximately in the middle of the 20,000 years between our present and the time of . Once man (with great difficulty) had won this war, which raged across the galaxy for a century, an edict was set forth which would thereafter govern man‚Äôs relation to technology (a Prime Directive, if you will): ‚ÄúThou shalt not make a machine in the likeness of a human mind.‚Äù





Some readers have voiced concerns about gender issues in Dune. It does portray a galactic ruling society in which males are the dominant rulers of the Great Houses, and have both wives and concubines. Well, it is a story. The author is not espousing this model as the way a society should be set up. The Bene Gesserit society of women have awesome mental powers and clearly have engineered much of the very long range plot line of the backstory, which comes to fruition in the novel. It is also made explicitly clear in the novel that both Leto and his son Paul revere and love the ‚Äúconcubine‚Äù women that bear their sons, and hold the official wives they might marry as merely political unions (along the lines of betrothals between royalty in ages past here on Earth).

So, even if you are repelled about male domination of females (which I am) I don't believe this novel will upset you.

‚ÄúThe Fremen have the word of Muad‚ÄôDib,‚Äù Paul said. ‚ÄúThere will be flowing water here open to the sky and green oases rich with good things. But we have the spice to think of, too. Thus, there will always be desert on Arrakis‚Ä¶and fierce winds, and trials to toughen a man. We Fremen have a saying: ‚ÄòGod created Arrakis to train the faithful.‚Äô One cannot go against the word of God.‚Äù

Rereading ‚ÄòDune‚Äô a couple of decades after I first encountered it as a teenager, I can still remember being swept up by its exoticism, mystery and action, a heady blend as addictive as the spice melange itself. But from the vantage point of 2021, an astonishing 56 years after its initial publication ‚Äì and it is currently still an Amazon bestseller ‚Äì it is clear that the very basis for the reputation, longevity and interpretation of ‚ÄòDune‚Äô is as mercurial as the shifting sands of Arrakis itself.

In an article from The Guardian entitled ‚ÄòDune, 50 years on: how a science fiction novel changed the world‚Äô (3 July 2015), Hari Kunzru comments that: ‚ÄúBooks read differently as the world reforms itself around them, and the Dune of 2015 has geopolitical echoes that it didn‚Äôt in 1965, before the oil crisis and 9/11.‚Äù Barely a decade later, and the world around us has irrevocably changed yet again. Kunzru elaborates:



I think the problem with ‚ÄòDune‚Äô is that it has become hermetically sealed by its own success, removed from the constraints of time and genre. Kunzru rightfully points out that Herbert‚Äôs own influences at the time were the Barsoom series by Edgar Rice Burroughs, the Lensman series by E.E. ‚ÄòDoc‚Äô Smith and, of course, the Foundation saga by Isaac Asimov, itself the subject of a new big-budget Apple+ television adaptation. These were the SF writers I was familiar with, and which shaped my view of the genre at the same time as I engaged with Herbert‚Äôs work. In this context, ‚ÄòDune‚Äô was but another iteration of that genre, and not the iconic object of veneration it has become today:



In terms of ‚Äòpolitical relevance‚Äô, it is clear that Denis Villeneuve is taking the proverbial bull by the horns with his latest adaptation. The second trailer reveals that the story is framed by Chani, who has experienced first-hand the brutal colonial subjugation of the Harkonnens, and who cynically wonders if the incoming Atreides will simply be their next oppressors.

This is in marked contrast to David Lynch using Princess Irulan to frame his 1984 adaptation, speaking with the hindsight of history, but still representing the ultimate expression of that colonialist mindset, as represented by her father the Emperor, the Landsraad, CHOAM, the Guild and, let us not forget, the Bene Gesserit itself.

Perhaps an unintended consequence of the Villeneuve trailer‚Äôs focus on the Paul/Chani relationship, obviously due to the marketing department honing in on the combined fan base of Timoth√©e Chalamet and Zendaya to generate buzz and success for the upcoming movie, is that it foregrounds the ‚Äòwhite saviour‚Äô theme that seems so troubling in ‚ÄòDune‚Äô, especially in our time where issues of race and gender are so highly politicised and emotive.

It is only troubling though if you have not read ‚ÄòDune Messiah‚Äô, where Herbert effectively and quite devastatingly turns the previous book on its head, taking the ‚Äòhero‚Äô story of Paul in a rather unexpected direction ‚Ä¶ a direction that becomes ever stranger and more baroque by the time we reach ‚ÄòGod Emperor of Dune‚Äô and ‚ÄòHeretics of Dune‚Äô, my two favourite books in the entire sequence.

What I found remarkable about this reread of ‚ÄòDune‚Äô (my third, I think) is how quick a read it is. The page counts among the various editions seem to vary as wildly as the reported length of Shai Hulud, ranging from 500 to 800. I think the former is more accurate. Anyway, the narrative races along at quite a furious clip. I must say it does slow down in the third section, ‚ÄòThe Prophet‚Äô, which does feel like a lot of filler as we build up towards the climactic battle.

Much has been said about how dire Herbert‚Äôs writing style is. Yes, most of the characters are cardboard cut-outs, with their thoughts and responses telegraphed in italicised speech that is the literary equivalent of speech bubbles in a graphic novel (speaking of which, ‚ÄòDune‚Äô now exists in that format as well, including a series of shorter comic books, if I‚Äôm not mistaken.)

What do we really know of Gurney Halleck and Duncan Idaho, apart from the broadest of writerly brush strokes? Even Paul himself does not convince, starting out as a callow 15-year-old and ending up defeating the Padishah Emperor himself. The women fare no better, with Mohiam repeatedly referred to as a ‚Äòcrone‚Äô or a ‚Äòwitch‚Äô, and a boilerplate reference to Chani‚Äôs ‚Äòdark elfin features‚Äô, which is meant to alert the reader to her ‚Äònative attractions‚Äô, I think.

No, what makes ‚ÄòDune‚Äô really stand out is the exemplary world-building. What struck me rereading this is how conditioned we have become, via the David Lynch and SyFy Channel adaptations in particular, to think of it as, well, a barren desert planet, when in fact it is a fully-fledged ecology. Herbert‚Äôs nature writing and scene setting here are quite painterly, giving us an intimate glimpse of the fauna and flora of Arrakis (yes, there are even flowers and birds!)

We now know, thanks to Kim Stanley Robinson and his Mars trilogy, that terraforming is not politically correct. But Herbert states quite clearly that the Fremen intuitively comprehend the intimate relationship between the desert, the worms and the spice. They only plan to terraform the more accessible parts, leaving the deep unchartered sands to Shai Hulud himself.

What Herbert does so skilfully in ‚ÄòDune‚Äô is to craftily use his action set pieces as disguised info dumps. Think of scenes like Liet Kynes taking the Duke and Paul via ornithopter to see spice mining for the first time. We the reader see this incredible spectacle through the eyes of our protagonists, experiencing what they are seeing and feeling, while learning about the planet‚Äôs intricate and dangerous symbiotic sandworm lifeforms at the same time.

Herbert similarly uses the Paul-versus-Jamis fight to educate the reader about Fremen rites and sociocultural practices, particularly as these relate to religion and death. Jamis‚Äôs burial is oddly elegiac and moving, a moment of great stillness and reflection in the wake of the desperate and lethal combat that preceded it. The Jamis fight is followed immediately by Feyd-Rautha Harkonnen celebrating his seventeenth birthday by dispatching his hundredth gladiator to an adoring crowd in an arena on Giedi Prime.

We learn early on in the book that the Baron is actually Paul‚Äôs
, but Herbert does not really do much with this. I wonder therefore if the Baron‚Äôs paedophelia-or-homosexuality (I don‚Äôt think it is really clear in which camp he falls), is but one end of a Kinsey Scale of a familial chain, with the depraved Harkonnens at one end and the rigidly moralistic Atreides at the other.

I was surprised to read somewhere that Frank Herbert had a complex relationship with a gay son that went unresolved until the latter‚Äôs death from AIDS in 1993 (Brian Herbert briefly refers to his brother in ‚ÄòDreamer of Dune‚Äô, his hagiography of his father.) However, I honestly don‚Äôt think one can read too much into this.

At the end of the day, ‚ÄòDune‚Äô remains as gloriously inscrutable and probably as batshit crazy as it must have done to its original readership. Actually, kudos to David Lynch‚Äôs screenplay, which manages to wrestle the beast into somewhat of a malleable form, despite such Star Trek liberties as the ‚Äòweirding modules‚Äô. And that closing shot of it raining on Arrakis.

The Villeneuve trailers to date have used much of the Lynchian iconography, such as the framing device and Paul‚Äôs visions of Chani and the Fremen, not to mention the trip to Arrakis in the Guild‚Äôs heighliners, which is a mere throwaway line later in the book. Integral characters like Chani and Stilgar appear in person only much later, and Lynch quite cleverly used Paul‚Äôs visions as a kind of linking device to splice the narrative together.

SOME THOUGHTS ON THE LATEST DUNE MOVIE ADAPTATION
When Villeneuve took on the project, he apparently stated he would only do so if he would be allowed to make two movies. That apocryphal story has since changed, with it being reported that no second movie has been greenlit, pending the success of the first one. As with ‚ÄòHarry Potter‚Äô and ‚ÄòLord of the Rings‚Äô, one would have thought that Warner Bros. would have given Villeneuve the go-ahead to write, develop and shoot the movies back-to-back to save costs and ensure continuity. The recent IMAX unveiling of the first ten minutes of footage did, apparently, feature a title card clearly stating ‚ÄòDUNE: PART 1‚Äô.

With a run time of 155 minutes, Villeneuve has also said that his movie only covers the ‚Äòfirst half‚Äô of the book. Current speculation as to the cut-off point is either Paul‚Äôs fight with Jamis or Jessica taking the Water of Life. The former seems more likely, although I do hope Villeneuve includes the elaborate funeral ceremony and subsequent insight into Fremen culture this allows. However, this probably does mean that Part 1 is likely to end on a rather subdued note if it goes full Jamis, so maybe the Water of Life is a better cliffhanger?

What I suspect though ‚Äì and there is an inkling of this in comments from some of the crew such as cinematographer Greig Fraser that Part 1 is entirely ‚Äòself-contained‚Äô ‚Äì is that Villeneuve has done his own ‚Äòtake‚Äô on ‚ÄòDune‚Äô that eschews many of the plot elements, action set pieces and even characters we are so familiar with. So come its release end October, we could be in for a rude awakening ‚Ä¶ (How much of a veil of secrecy is likely to be cast on its screening at the Venice Film Festival remains to be seen.)

positive

This book is HEAVY! I am behind the times on this classic novel. I went in the wrong direction due to a hugely long waitlist to check this book out. I watched the most recent movie and then read the book. Honestly, I thought the movie did a pretty good job. The main issue with this book is that it is SOOOOOO long. It should have been split in two.

The third book section and beyond felt less developed and edited. There were a lot of newer concepts, and the relationships were extremely rushed. I wonder if Frank Herbert originally intended this to be a single novel and was trying to wrap up the ending.

With that being said. This book is epic as F. You must read it. The beginning is very Sci-Fi heavy, but after they are established on the new planet, it reads more like an epic fantasy. The characters have immense development. And somehow, I still wanted more of the culture.

Worth a read at least once in your life.

4-4.5 Stars. I will definitely continue on with the series.

Intrigue Imbroglio! Conspiracy or Conniption!: "Well, The Emperor tried one way, the Harkonnens another, the Bene Gesserit schemed a third way, the Guild a fourth way, - and yet - a desert mouse spanked them all!" - The Fremen Bugle.

An awesome novel of intrigue over action, as the youthful Paul Atreides encounters a nearly overwhelming destiny and rises above it by preserving his essential moral core.

P.S. A bit of a manual on power, manipulation, and intrigue in a sci-fi setting.

P.P.S. Do not try Bene Gesserit voice controls at home, I can attest that they don't actually work...(much to my dismay!)

P.P.P.S. I was equally dismayed by the fact that 'the Force,' didn't work from me either... I'm beginning to think that sci-fi authors are just making it all up...

~~~
neutral

BR with my Emma's Party friends (:
-----------------------

Liz against the world's rating: 3.9 Stars

Look at me, here I am reading Hugo's winner Dune 50 years after its publication and living to tell the tale. Dune is, without doubt, a groundbreaking story with a complex and enthralling universe. The concept of Dune is exhilarating: sandworms, a messiah, the Fremen and their spice.

The actual reading, however, is a slightly less perfect experience.



Princess Irulan states from the beginning that precocious Paul is not only meant for greatness, he's already a Legend. So Dune is not much about Paul struggling to believe he's the One as it is about Paul in his heroic journey fighting his fate: the unstoppable Fremen jihad he so much wants to avoid. We can see him time after time refusing to be what his prescience has showed him.

And on that note,The funny thing is, Herbert keeps telling the story as if you haven't already been warned.




When you talk about Dune, you have to admit the world-building is flawless but when it comes to character development, time-skip is its biggest enemy and mine: I'm a characters reader. And even if the characters were on spot most of the time,

There is little to no characterization maybe because Herbert indulges his interest in ecology, political philosophy, religion and the messianic quest more than anything else which, if I understand correctly,

I didn't get to see the actual of the characters and so there was a lot of guessing for me: about Fremen as a society, Paul and Chani's relationship, his son, and more. It's a page turner, alright, but because you're in painful need to make sense of the story. And you kinda love it, honestly.



Despite all this, I'm completely captivated by Herbert's creative world. I couldn't get enough of Paul's ability to see present, past and future as a now, the pseudo-technology, the Bene Gesserit and of course, the spice and its properties to name a few.

All in all, it was a great book and I enjoyed a lot of it even if I fought with Herbert through the whole experience. I'm not sure if I'll keep up with the series, though, but I'm sure fans of the genre will absolutely love this.

~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

~~~
positive
~~~
I read this book the first time last year and I absolutely loved it. I loved it so much I had to reread it again this year but this time I'm tackling the rest of the series too.

My review of the book from last year is down below:



This book was awesome!! Not only was the adventure amazing but the growth of the characters in this story is something every writer can learn from to make their own characters more real. The desert planet setting had a life of it's own. It was believable. Herbert's talent as a writer is undeniable. There is a reason Dune is one of the greatest epics of science fiction.

Paul Atreides/Muad'Dib/Kwisatz Haderach is an amazing character. Originally groomed to be the Duke of Caladan by his father Leto, he and his family are sent away by the emperor to a desert planet called Arrakis, or Dune. Paul's life is overturned by treachery from those the family trusted most. On Dune Paul must learn to survive the harsh environment of this Tatooine-like planet and perhaps one day avenge the traitorous plot against his noble family.

It surprises me how some of the greatest books are set in desert-like settings. In Star Wars the main body of the story is set on Tatooine, a desert planet. The Way of Kings is mostly set on the Shattered Plains where it is dry and arid (though not as devoid of water as Dune or Tatooine). The symbology of it is awesome.

I also found it interesting how Herbert weaves so much symbology, anthropology, and moral lessons into this story. I felt like I was reading an ethnography of the Fremen peoples. By the end I knew who they were, what they ate, what their religion was, how they buried their dead, and so on. This book was richer because of Herbert's careful planning and research. I found it very cool how the people even save the water from their breath, and even from the dead, because in all deserts water is sacred.

The ritual of how Paul got the name Muad'Dib, reminded me how in some cultures the giving of a name is just as important as one's birth. Muad'Dib means mouse. I liked that. Its interesting how different groups perceive things differently. We look at a mouse and think of a critter that gets into our house or carries disease, but in Fremen culture they are seen as "wise in the ways of the desert. Muad'Dib creates his own water. Muad'Dib hides from the sun and travels in the cool night. Muad'Dib is fruitful and multiplies over the land. Muad'Dib we call 'instructor-of-boys.' That is a powerful base on which to build your life, Paul-Muad'Dib, who is Usul among us. We welcome you." Its interesting to think that through the eyes of the Fremen they look at the mouse as wise and something they try to mimic. Its funny how people find wisdom. Probably why traveling the world and seeing the world through the eyes of others only adds to our life the those lives around us.

In conclusion I would definitely recommend this book, not only because it was the first to win the Nebula Award, shared the Hugo Award, and undoubtedly set the stage for science fiction, but it is a story we can all relate to and it shows how nothing is more important than family.

A masterpiece that still stands up today due to the strength of the author's characterisations.
Dune is a totally immersing reading experience ; the level of world building on show is truly incredible.
An epic book that has a lot of space within the writing that leaves the imagination to do the work. For instance ; there are no big descriptions of set piece battles. The readers experience of them is all told second hand from the view point of those who were not necessarily directly involved. This should prove exciting from the new movies perspective as it looks like the director Denis Villneuve's has given his own imagination free reign in bringing Dune to the screen, while still staying faithful to the book. (Excellent article and interviews in this months SFX magazine,by the way) .A wonderful must read Sci-fi epic and I can't wait for the movie to come out.

~~~
positive
~~~
Dune is one of those classics that I've long been aware of, but never really felt like tackling. I remember bits and pieces from the 1984 movie, and I've definitely heard sci-fi lovers wax all poetic about the books. Yet with over 800 pages in the first book, setting time aside for it always felt like such a chore.

Then I went to watch the 2021 movie, spent over 2 hours admiring Zendaya's soulful blue eyes, and fangirling over Jessica's creepy Bene Gesserit voice... only to realize at the end, that not much of the world made sense to me. So with frustration prevailing, I finally picked up the first book.



The story is simple enough: 15-year-old heir to the House of Atreides, Paul, finds himself running
for his life on the desert planet of Arrakis. With most of his family murdered, his mother in grieving mood more often than not, the young boy is forced to rely on his budding (and extremely confusing) prescient abilities to survive.

The events are powered by a complex mix of politics and religion, with a liberal sprinkling of ecological theories. I loved the politics, especially the chapters describing the enemy tactics, and the ecological details painted a very interesting picture of the desert planet. The religious aspects however, were not to my taste. Admittedly, I do have some very deeply-rooted personal bias when it comes to this topic, so I may be looking at things more critically than others.



Even so, with enough foreshadowing, I think I may have eventually warmed up to the whole prescient Messianic Child hype. Additionally, I had the bright idea to put the book on hold for a couple of weeks, in order to catch up with other books from my reading goal. And when I came back, I was never able to immerse myself in quite the same way.

That said, I'd still attribute a big part of my disappointing experience to the story's sudden penchant for time skips: years, months, weeks you name it. The last 80 pages have them in spades. Though I did occasionally find the day by day approach tedious, the sudden shift in pacing was too jarring.



And worst of all, the time skips didn't feel entirely justified. Or perhaps I felt the need for a few more bridging phrases, especially for that first 3-year time skip. You can't just put everything on fast-forward, set significant character development between scenes, also expect the reader to get emotional during the final scenes.



On the whole, watching both the movie and reading the book was a good choice. The former shows a more human side of 15-year-old Paul, while the latter fills in the gaps from the world-building.

~~~
positive
~~~
Listening to the audio production made this an enjoyable experience. I had tried to read Dune twice before and could not get through it. The last time was many years ago. I might have been able to now, as an older adult, but I wanted to finish so I thought an audiobook with a full cast was the way to go. I'm glad I did it this way.

I will fully acknowledge that Dune must have been WAY ahead of its time. Sort to like LOTR was for epic fantasy. And yeah, I would also call it a classic, a masterpiece, pick your favorite label. They would apply. The story is absolutely epic. I like the mix of tech and mysticism. The mind and creativity that came up with this is nothing short of astounding.



Yeah it does present some difficulties. At least it did for me and I could very much be off base on some of these points, so take all this with a grain of salt. It does have a lot of world building. I normally really, really like that. But here it was pretty dense. I don't know how else to put it. It also seemed to have large jumps in time (unless I'm missing something) where there was significant events that basically I had to try to infer. Like Paul having kids? One moment he's developing feelings for the Fremen woman, the next "part" of the book starts and he's got kids. Maybe I missed something in there. And then there seemed to be a jump where they were gearing up for a war on Arrakis, and then, again, a new "Part" starts and the war had been raging for some time. It just seemed odd to me to skip over much quickly. It also seemed odd to me that they have space travel, interstellar and an illusion box that can make it look like your hand is burning, but to me it sounded like most of the combat (other than nukes) was with knives. Interesting.

And here's the one I'm probably most going to get rapped in the mouth for. I wasn't, candidly, all that crazy about Paul. I get that he's noble-born and all that and he's in conflict with a family that is trying to wipe him and his out. But even with his own guys, sometimes he came across as kind of haughty.

But I honestly liked it enough to get the audio (if there is one) for the next book. Maybe someday I'll try re-reading a hard copy too. Seems to me this is one of those books that you would get more from each time you read it. Certainly is that way for me with LOTR.

positive
~~~
This is a masterpiece of world building. Admittedly, this will not appeal to everyone.

Does the world need another Dune review? I very much doubt it needs mine but that never stopped me before, saturation be damned!

Dune. A single word that conjures images of sandworms, spice wars, great battles between rival dynastic families and a massively detailed and intricately crafted universe. No wonder this is widely regarded as not just a Science Fiction masterpiece, but a literary achievement as well.

The appeal of Dune is peculiar. All authors create little worlds in their stories but Herbert created a world. He doesn't just say that Arrakis is a desert planet, he engrosses himself and the reader into the geology. We get a new planet, and while it's obvious that the author based his desert planet on real sights and cultures, it's still awesome. The Fremen and their culture, the stillsuits, the worms, the plight for water... It's all explained in such a vivid detail without being boring or repetitive. And while reading you can see the references!

Is it space opera? Is it political commentary? Is it philosophical exploration? Is it fantasy? _Dune_ is all of these things and possibly more. One thing I do know: it's a I highly recommend it to any and all. I must admit that there is the occasional infelicity in some of Herbert's prose (and a too-heavy reliance on inner monologues to either state the obvious or convey information to the reader), but overall I can forgive him this for having crafted such an excellent tale. Woven into the story of a tottering space empire are real questions about ecology, responsibility and human life that are well-worth thinking about. Politics is not just a veneer, but the lifeblood of this story and, to me at least, it makes it all the more exciting.

All of these incredibly ambitious elements are all tightly woven together. Take out one element and the story loses its cohesion. Despite all the ridiculous amounts of detail there is nothing extraneous in this novel.

~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

~~~
positive
~~~
I love this book. This was my first re-read of the classic that I'm sure I'll visit with again a few times down the road. But I shouldn't have waited 20 years. This book is too good to go that long without reading again.

How can I do it justice in a review? I can say, "it's cool", but that doesn't come close. Even a 5-star rating seems to be short of expressing its worth. This is one of those books that should be allowed a 6th star.

I can't even pin down what it is exactly that makes this stand out. Sure, it's got great literary elements, likable and sympathetic heroes, wickedly delicious villains, awesome "monsters" in the sandworms, a science fiction technology system that makes sense. It has epic "universe building", a plausible "magic" system in the Bene Gesserit sisterhood. It has old style swordplay and futuristic intergalactic scope.

I've always said it's a science fiction that reads like a fantasy. The technology is truly secondary. Once everyone gets to Arrakis, the fantastic elements take over.

Herbert's prose is what makes the difference, I think. He tells an engaging story with an easy going clear language. His work is layered and has so much in it: philosophy, religion, science, morality, loyalty, cruelty, politics, espionage. It leaves the reader thinking long after closing the book. Reading Herbert makes me feel like I've learned something yet all the while I was being entertained.

~~~
positive
~~~
I don't know where to start with this book and explaining it to anyone, it's clearly a masterpiece.

It was first published in 1965, it's just way ahead of it's time; nothing like I've read from the 60s before and nothing like I've ever read before in general.

God knows how long it took the author to write this, you can definitely tell it's carefully written; it's just full of depth, details of the characters and surroundings.

There's a lot of politics in this book, which doesn't interest me much and it does drag from time to time but it doesn't stop me one bit from giving it a top rating because like I said - it's just a masterpiece and the writing is genius.

Personally I think to fully digest this book, it has to be read two or three times because it is the most complex book I've ever read; if you've read it you'll know exactly what I mean.

I'm aware of the mixed reviews on the sequels, so I'm a little weary of continuing; what's your opinion on them?

~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

~~~
positive
~~~
Since I got into reading (about 3 years now), this book always came up. Whether on YouTube or Goodreads. I always wanted to read it, but always thought, from what I saw other reviewers saying, that it is very hard to follow and quite confusing. So I dropped the idea.
Then the Villenueve movie came out. It looked really good (and since this guy directed Blade Runner 2049, it was practically guaranteed to at least be a good story).
I liked the movie. It gave a good sense of immorality, politics and identity. I found myself being a bit bored at times, I think for the reason that I was kind of confused. Like, where was the world building?
I wasn't planning on reading this, at least until the next movie came out, but a fellow Goodreads friend told me you'll love the movie more if you read the book (thanks Allison). I saw it at a store, and listened to her advice.
And oh brother, am I glad I did.

Right off the bat, I knew this would at least be a three star read. The politics were interesting and the world was mysterious. Some said the start was confusing, which I totally get, though understood what was happening because I had already watched the movie. B even though I watched the film before hand, I still enjoyed it as if I never had. I'm actually glad I did, because I didn't feel lost like so many other readers seem to be.
The movie starts exactly like the book, just dumping you into the world, no world building or nothing (though this certainly doesn't stay that way for long). Now that I think about it, there really wasn't a time where Herbert info-dumped, yet I knew the world as well as I could. That is talent, and something I love in Sci-Fi.

The setting is perfect. Seriously. Anything desert related, whether it be movies or books, I love. Especially now that it is set in the future. Water is sparse. They can't even cry...ah! I love it. Herbert created a very fascinating indigenous group, and also somehow found a way for me to feel anxious while I was reading.
Water! Don't run out of water!
The Fremen seemed so in control of everything, but honestly I think I would hang myself if I had to live in that tribe. The thought of not having water is so scary. And I need showers, else I could, quite literally, go bonkers.

"Deep in the human unconsciousness is a pervasive need for a logical universe that
makes sense. But the real universe is always one step beyond logic"
-"The Sayings of Muad'Dib" by the Princess Irulan.

The Harkonnens were awesome enemies. The Baron was so gross, though. Which I guess is a good trait in a villain. They really did him well in the film (can we please appreciate that acting and make up?!). Actually, come to think of it, the casting for the movie is exceptionally done. Rebecca Ferguson is probably my favourite actress, and I think she did a great job with Jessica, especially at the start of the book.
The characters aren't exactly charismatic. With that being said, I did still like Paul, Leto and Jessica a fair amount. The side characters might be better than the main ones. My favourites were Gurney and Stilga. Just know that characters are not the strong point of this book...


...unlike the world-building. Herbert created such an incredible world that is truly jaw dropping, as you know you haven't even made a dint in the lore and politics. It is just so creative. Very atmospheric and majestic, some of best and most impressive I've read. I don't really understand the readers who say that the world-building is non-existent throughout the book. It is very much present, and very well done. If you are a world-building nerd, you'll love this, as the world was just so incredible. Things like the 'thopters were so creative (as well as the sandworms). Such unique concepts, even to todays standards. That is another thing that is amazing about this book; it is very unique. Way ahead of its time. When I was reading it, I was like "You really just thought of that, Frank? Blue eyes? Worlds controlled by spice? Bug looking helicopters? C'mon, man." Really though, if the director does this next film right, they could become among the greatest Sci-Fi movies of the of all time. And plus the first one stayed very close to the book. Don't chuck a Peter Jackson on us, Dennis! Am definitely looking forward to it.

Before I get into some very tiny cons, I have to discuss the messages this book puts forth. The way it presented these ideas--through economical, political and religious power--was very thought provoking. All the characters in this book uses the above powers to hurt people for their own benefit. It is honestly disruptive when you think about how it resembles things in our society. How so much human suffering has come from political power designed to manipulate the people, as well as how some figures rise to power. There are just...so many things in this book to look deep into and discuss, practically begging a reread (which I'll certainly do at some point). But man, it really was thought-provoking, in more ways than what I mentioned (not to mention the innumerable philosophical aspects).

Two cons for me, would be:

1. The writing is so strange. I feel like I could never capture the setting or the scenery properly because it just wasn't explained right! Might just be me, but it annoyed me, on multiple occasions, how I never knew whereabouts the character were and what it looks like around them!

2. The middle of Book III was a bit boring. It took me awhile to get through it, but the ending climax was very much worth it. (Damn, Paul, why you gotta do Rautha like that?).

But, that's really it. I loved this book. I don't mean that lightly, I adored it. Those issue above are just small nitpicks, so I am still going to give it an easy 5 stars. It is just an awesome work of literature. Slow, complex and majestic, this is a must-read not just for Sci-Fi fans, but all readers. A book for the ages.
