neutral
~~~
3.5/5 Stars


I'm glad I got over my fear of reading this book


Ever since I started reading again, I've seen thatDune had been a book that is a MUST read. And I've always been scared to pick it up because it's a long, classic sci-fi novel. I can finally say I conquered ! Unfortunately, I think I hyped this book up too much for myself...

Let me start by saying this: Parts 1 & 2 were amazing! Part 1 was a lot of getting to know the setting, the plot, and the characters. It had great dialogue, intriguing concepts, and an explosive end. Part 2 had a shift in pacing and was slower, but was fascinating to read from and got a lot of character development. Part 3 is where this book really tanks it for me. It was honestly gruelling to get through. It felt like I was reading a totally different book! I wasn't really happy with how some of the characters' arcs played out and I wasn't satisfied with the ending in any way. It was just a huge let down!

I really enjoyed how water is seen a sacred currency (priceless, even) in this book. The environmental topics and themes that Herbert incorporated here pose really thought provoking discussions and I loved reading about them.

The character in the first two acts were so engaging and I really cared for them, especially Lady Jessica. I loved seeing a bad ass woman have power over these men! BUT, for some reason, I feel that Herbert SEVERELY under-used her potential. Her character is quite literally one of the most powerful characters in the story, and yet I still felt that she wasn't used to her full capacity (maybe this is rectified in later instalments).

On that note, I wanted to talk about one of the common criticisms of this novel: the women. I've seen that a lot of people didn't think that the women in this novel were treated with much respect or that they were written poorly. At first, I didn't see that because Lady Jessica was first written to be this uber powerful woman. And then came the ending of Part 2 and all of Part 3, where the women just seemed to be concubines for the men, despite their actual power ranks. Even Chani, who is the other strong female character, gets pushed to the background in Part 3. How people talked to the women were starting to really get on my nerves! Just didn't make sense.

Another of the criticisms I read was that Herbert was appropriating Arabic cultures. And that criticism became more apparent as you work your way through the novel. The language, the societal and cultural norms as well as religious beliefs seemed like they were taken from typical stereotypes of Arabic cultures, which felt really off to me.

The last thing I'll criticize is the actual plot. When I finished this book, I was like, , which I can say is my own fault due to lack of research on if this book could stand on its own (It can't. It has an abrupt ending). The first 3 quarters of this book was so interesting and juicy to read, but now it just all feels like set up for the next books.

Anyways, this review is getting long enough lol. I hope that the movie can correct these problems that are apparent in the book. I still think this book does have its place in history and is extremely influential. While reading it, I couldn't help but see its influence in things like and even . If you like slow builds and politics in your books, then possibly check this one out.

‚ÄúI must not fear. Fear is the mind-killer. Fear is the little-death that brings total obliteration. I will face my fear. I will permit it to pass over me and through me. And when it has gone past I will turn the inner eye to see its path. Where the fear has gone there will be nothing. Only I will remain.‚Äù

Dune can be easily listed as one of the most difficult books, that I have ever read. Under the guise of a science fiction, this book can easily be classified as a treatise on existentialism, philosophy, politics, region, war manual(?) I honestly don't have the capability to "review" this cult classic.
My only dilemma is

Should I rate this magnum opus for its sheer brilliance? the extent of themes and issues that his book deals with? all the philosophical discourse that forms an inherent core of the story? Or should I rate this book for the author's imagination for giving us this story, progressive to such an extent that it wont grow old even if one reads it in the next decade or four?

But, how do I reconcile to the fact that I hated the protagonist right from the very beginning? No matter how much I tried, I never warmed up to him. Nevertheless, the other characters kind of made up for my irritation towards him.

Will I read the sequel? No, not yet.
Why? Tbh, I didn't even get half the things, that this one set out to portray. There are so many subtle, hidden nuances within this book, I need to read it again, to fully understand it.
So a

P.S.: I partially read the book and part listened to the audio. The audio production requires a special mention here. Its one of audio books I've read till date.

neutral
~~~
3.4‚≠ê Updates Review & Rating
It's been four decades plus since I first read Frank Herbert's sprawling, shambling, quasi-mystical, soap opera of a science fiction epic. Any review of Dune the novel now has to take into account Dune the phenomenon. Three movies, a miniseries and numerous prequels/sequels by Herbert and his inheritors have etched this tale into popular culture. Bottom line though; it's just a good story.

Some main elements appear well rooted in western literary tradition, from King Arthur and Robin Hood (evil Barons!) to Greek mythology; others reflect Middle Eastern religious and political influences ( Islam and the Ottoman Empire). Re-reading the book made me realize how 60's it is and how it resonates with another science fiction classic of the time, Heinlein's Stranger in a Strange Land. That's certainly not to say the novel is dated, it just reflects the ambiance of the era in which it was written.

My older self has more patience with and more appreciation of Herbert's dive into religous and cultural themes, embodied by the Freman ( where younger me just wanted to get back to killing Harkonnens). I don't see any point in recapping the now familiar plot but clearly Herbert's vivid descriptions of his settings and his characters have kept his tale fresh for 50 years. Bottom line though; it's just a good story.

2023 Re-read
I stand by what I said in the 2021 review (above), mostly. But I dropped the rating half a star because Herbert's psuedo mystical ramblings about Paul's supposed psychic powers did get to be too much, this time. I'm getting old and cranky, I suppose.
Still a good story, though.

~~~
Everyone who enjoys Star Trek or Star Wars probably owes a huge debt to Frank Herbert. Dune preceded both of them, and was the first sci-fi book to prove that sci-fi could appeal to the masses, not just a small group. It is foundation, and it fathered a plethora of copy-cats and sincere imitators. In that regard, I bow to Mr. Herbert. It was interesting enough to make me follow through to the end...I wanted to know how Paul would pull this off, where it would leave him, and what would happen to all those other interesting characters we began the book with. However, it is proof positive that this just isn‚Äôt my genre. I do not want, in the least, to read any other book in the series.

There is more at the heart of this book than just a battling galaxy or a struggle for control of a planet. It is about ecology, power, corruption, betrayal and all those other ugly things people are always either pushing or battling. It is about one class trying to control another, and it skirts the subjects of religion and spirituality. But, for me, it never reached the level of making any profound statements on any of those subjects. It was much like watching one of those Star Trek movies it reminded me of, a bit of fun that isn‚Äôt likely to affect you to the point of hindering your ability to sleep.

~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

~~~
positive
~~~
What can I to say about this one? This was some formative sci-fi in my youth and while the details are murky the sentiment was clear - I loved this as a kid. It's been on the want to re-read shelf forever but you know how it is with things you loved as a child. I mean have you tried to watch Dukes of Hazzard as an adult? You wonder what kind of a moron you were in grade school.

The Atreides aren't just some good ole boys, never meaning no harm (my God, what sort of a hole have I dug myself here) and Dune did not disappoint. The first half had me hooked and I would have happily plodded along as House Atreides settled on Arrakis. I loved the political intrigues, the backroom dealings, and the strategies inside strategies. I gobbled up the pseudo-religious talk and the historical notes and read it again as an ecological warning.

I generally don't read these sort of sprawling epics and even now I'm hesitant to read any more of the series (though it looks like I'm in relatively good hands with the first trilogy) Even if my time with the Fremen ends here, this was just the sort of indulgent clunker of a classic that I needed right now and can only hope that Denis Villeneuve once again manages to film the unfilmable.

~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

~~~
positive
~~~
This is one of those books that I've always thought that I should read, but never actually wanted to read, simply because I thought that it would have to be tedious and dry and, I hate to say it, boring. Which goes to show what a poor book-cover judge I am, because this book was anything but tedious, dry or boring. In fact, one of the first things that struck me about this book was the readability and fast-paced action and intrigue. So much happened in such a short amount of time, that I'd have to go back and read sections over again to be sure that I understood everything that had happened. Good stuff.

I have to say that I loved Herbert's writing style. It's deceptively simple and to the point... until 10, or 30, or 100 pages later when you realize that what seemed so simple and unimportant was a set-up for a revelation later, and your brain (or mine, at least) has this little "AHA!!" moment, and you feel so smart for figuring out exactly what you were so expertly and subtly led to figure out. Just imagine the possibilities for multiple readings... This book is one which can be read a dozen times and still reveal hidden nuggets of goodness.

Herbert's world, or universe, was so intriguing to me. I loved the political structure of having major and minor power families, an emperor, a 3rd party Guild to manage trade, and the 3rd party Bene Gesserit women, who sort of control-prophesy-manipulate to reach an end. The skills of the people inhabiting Herbert's universe are incredible, and so much based on mind-control. Not necessarily power over another's mind, although there is that, but I mean control over your OWN mind, to the point where instinctive reactions and involuntary bodily functions can be subverted and held in check, simply by will.

I loved Dune, as in the planet Arrakis, and the people who inhabited it, except of course for the Harkonnen jerk-faces. The Fremen are interesting and resourceful and bad-ass and wise, and are able to accept their lot, while trying to make a better lot for future generations. We could take a page out of their books, I think. We should be improving our planet, ensuring its inhabitability for as long as we're able (at least until the sun implodes and kills us all), but instead, we're polluting with reckless abandon, as if the planet is able to just reset with each generation.

Herbert's characters are some of the most interesting that I've read about as well. I loved that he infused a clear-cut Good vs. Bad struggle with deviousness and subtlety, and then on top of that, threw in characters that had to make choices that left you wondering who was real and whether they acted of their own accord or if there was something more... I loved the Atreides. They were, to me, the pinnacle of honor-bound deviousness. That seems strange to say, but I mean it in the best way possible. They were devious only to try to detect and prevent deviousness against them, and to right wrongs that have gone on for generations of animosity between houses. I do wish that there was a reason given that the Atreides house was singled out for this treatment, but reading between the lines, it seems that greed and power-mongering was the cause, and the Atreides were honest enough to stand against it, and so became the enemy.

This book is one that has a great many moral and religious and life-lesson undertones, which is a fine line to walk in any book for me, as I really, really dislike being lectured to. But I think that Herbert handled this all very well, and I was able to read it without feeling as though there was a wagging finger in my face.

Overall, I really, really enjoyed this book. The only reason that I am not able to give this 5 stars is that I felt like the entire book was leading up to a spectacular ending... but the ending drug on for just a bit too long and wasn't as spectacular as I'd hoped that it would be. It was a good ending to a very good book, but I felt that it just could have been a little bit more.

I definitely recommend this book though. It's entertaining and informative and prescient and timely as well as timeless. Just go read it already. :)

~~~
positive
~~~
I will say this straight away - DUNE might just be one of the favorite books I have ever read. It is undeniably one of the most influential Sci-Fi novels to exist and I will recommend that most people should at least try to read it - it might just surprise you as it did me.

I will keep specific details on the actual novel very sparse apart from a few great quotes as that is how I entered it and I believe that is the best way.



The very first thing that compelled me was the writing itself. Dune is contemplative by nature - it makes the reader think with every word on every page. The novel is filled to the brim with quotable lines that tend to stay with you. I was actually surprised by how much I liked the writing since I was quite intimidated by the book before starting it but it turned out there was nothing too difficult there. In fact, I would say it reads better than many modern novels.



The next thing that immediately kicks you with awesomeness is the worldbuilding. Dune is stellar in building up cultures and religions. It can be a little heavy-handed in the beginning with all the terms and expressions but I promise that if you pay attention and just let it all flow over you then you will deeply appreciate everything Frank Herbert has done with building up Arrakis and the broader world of Dune. The novel is also littered with awesome epigraphs that do a great job of giving you both hints about things to come and a better overview of things the reader has already experienced.



The overall journey that the book follows is magnificent. The novel is comprised of a lot of dialogue, politics, and tension but it retains movement at the same time. It is done extremely well in my opinion although if books that focus solely on these things rarely compel you then you may have varying levels of enjoyment with the work. I thought it was grand and seeing our main character progress through the novel was pretty damn interesting. Something that really elevated the journey for me was the use of the third person omniscient POV. I had some fears before starting my readthrough but it just works so well when applied to a book with tons of political maneuvering. It is really interesting to see what everyone is thinking in a setting that focuses on backstabbing and intrigue.



And with this, my gushing review just about comes to an end. There are plenty more things to say about this book although 99% of them point to the same conclusion:

It is a magnificent piece of fiction and will forever be a book that I will cherish.

positive

Like many, my relationship with this science-fiction classic has changed as my life has progressed: too dense for me when first attempted as a young teen, by the time I was an undergraduate it was one of my all-time favorite novels; but then while reading it again near the age of 40, found a lot more problems with it than I had before, and more parts that made me roll my eyes and quietly laugh. Maybe this is why so many people over history have enjoyed the first novel but never read any of the rest of the saga? It's definitely a great book, for those who have never read it before: a combination of elaborate cultural backstory (ala JRR Tolkien), the far-flung future of humanity (ala Asimov's Foundation series), and a grand Eastern-influenced vision that evokes "Lawrence of Arabia," the original novel combines a Shakespearean tale of family intrigue with the trippy '60s elements of alternate realities and messiah-figure destiny. But yeah, let's face it; the older you are, the faster you'll be skipping over the pages upon of ponderous purple prose on display here, muttering to yourself the whole time, "Okay, okay, I get it, . Now what happens next?"

I really enjoyed reading Dune, I‚Äôd fall asleep thinking about it and dream of desert landscapes. I expected to be confused by this book, but I found it fairly easy to understand and follow. So if fear of confusion is the only thing holding you back, then ignore that fear. FEAR IS THE MIND-KILLER!

I didn‚Äôt know much about this story aside from: science, sandstorms, and giant worms. And I was surprised by how much I liked Paul, even though he wasn‚Äôt a character I would generally expect to like. I don‚Äôt want to spoil anything, but the similarities between Dune and a certain Disney movie is uncanny.

I will say that I didn‚Äôt love the ending. Frank Herbert did a great job of knowing when to take his time telling the story and when to jump ahead in the story‚Äôs timeline to effectively keep the reader interested. But the ending felt like it was rushed, it took too long for us to reach this point in the story, and when it finally did it was over in the snap of your fingers.

Loved this to bits, but couldn't help remarking the similarities between Dune and Wheel of Time:
- Paul Atreides is similar to Rand: an expected yet dreaded Messiah, who has powers that are usually only available to women; also he is prophesized to lead people to Jihad (Paul) / Breaking of the World (Rand);
- The Fremen are very much alike the Aiel, tough and very good warriors, with sister-wives and a very united community (plus a slight similarity between Jamis and Couladin);
- The Bene Gesserit reminded me heavily of the Aes Sedai, not just because of the witchery, but especially because of the scheming.

positive
~~~
This is my Book Of the Month- January 2017, with GR group- Nothing But Reading Challenges- Category: Adult Sci-Fi BOM.

This book was originally published in 1965 and is considered a Science Fiction Classic. I am very surprised that I hadn't heard about it before it got picked as part of a Group Read. I am glad I decided to go ahead with this read and if nothing else, this book is one of it's kind.

A future world, where there is intergalactic politics, mixed with some magic. Even though this is a future world, there is no major technology, instead we have feel of reading a historical book which is set in the future. This was definitely a unique way of writing a story.



We have 15 yr old Paul Atreides (the heir apparent to Duke Leto Atreides and heir of House Atreides) along with his parents moving to the Desert Planet Arrakis (also known as Dune) to rule and attempt to bring about civilization. This is where the real story kicks-off and one act of betrayal spirals towards a Prophecy being fulfilled.


It's for us to find out, what kind of Ruler would Paul actually be.

In Dune, Freeman are people living in the core of the desert. I read somewhere that the writer took reference from Tuareg tribe and Bedouin tribe to draft their living style.


Then there is the spice and the water, natural resources that play a very heavy hand and are responsible for a lot of major decisions by all the important characters in this book.

And as the story progresses we see that everything revolves around politics and religion.




--> The writing is really heavy and I had to re-read sentences to get the context in some places. There are lot of terminology that were new (part of the Dune world) and took some time getting used to.

--> The ending felt a bit rushed, Paul growing up years were kind of skipped and we reached the climax of the story. The first half of the book was slower. Maybe it could have been balanced a bit better.

--> Princess Irulan is the narrator of the story


Even after the above mentioned problems, it would be a shame to give this anything less than a 4 star because overall this story is really really good.

As part of the group discussion I realized that there is a movie based on the book. I sure have got to watch it now.

neutral
~~~
As kids, my older brother was a Dune guy, whereas I bowed at the altar of John Ronald Reuel‚Äîand neither of us could ever bring ourselves to meet fully in the other's territory. I have tried Frank Herbert's renowned series several times now but have yet to make it further than Dune Messiah, the succinct, but inferior, follow-up to his smash-hit series opener. Everything that constitutes this curiously prescient science-fiction champion appeals to me: an alluringly thoughtful and flush aeon-spanning back story; a desert planet from which is harvested the priceless spice that allows interplanetary travel; feuding and scheming noble houses within a middle-eastern-flavored interstellar Empire; human computers and gaberdine sects that have replaced forbidden machines; the super-cool , the soldier-fanatics of the regnant Padishah-Emperor; the young Duke with mysterious portents and genetic mysteries interwoven about his young stud self and his formidable mystic mother, and so on and so forth. It's all for something to scratch all those itches and stoke all those fires and hoist itself up onto the same level as Tolkien's epic saga.

Except that it didn't.

Every time that I have returned to read this novel, I marvel at how much the first two-thirds suck me in, ramping up the tension and tautening the various strands of the conspiracy-bound story‚Äîand the introduction of the Fremen and their austere, feral, and ritualized Neo-Bedouin culture developed amidst an endlessly severe and brutal wasteland is the most brilliant component of this story that has several. And every time I can't believe that Herbert put all of that thought and imagination and effort into this puppy only to sink it with that terribly rushed and anti-climactic ending. With whiplash haste the Baron is toast, his nephew is skewered, the Emperor is debased, the Sardaukar humbled, the Fremen ascendant, and Paul Atreides the smiling blue-eyed boy of the moment. It's the same undue haste and palmball that he uses to hopelessly mar the ending of the already flawed . The tangled plots and machinations and generational maneuvering and betrayals and...THE END! PLOTS UNRAVELED! BAD GUYS SMOKED! BLAME PLACED ON MEDDLING KIDS NOTED! NEXT!

It's quite obvious that I'm in the miniscule minority here‚Äîand I've heard similarly vehement complaints in a reverse manner from my brother concerning the good Professor‚Äîbut the palpable disappointment I feel at what I consider Herbert's lamentable inability to finish a novel at near the same level with which he constructs the back story and fleshes out the opening sections becomes so intense that it bums me right the hell out. What's more, with nearly every reader warning me that the series gets progressively more obscure and turgid the further down the chain of sequels one ventures, I have never mustered the will to crack the spine of , let alone the infamous . Ah, Frank, you brilliant innovator and miscegenetic culture breeder, how magical it have been if only you had the moxie to finish what you started.

~~~
positive
~~~
2021 reread

It's been a decade since I last read this book, but I wanted to revisit it before the upcoming film. I'll tell you up front, I'm probably not an unbiased reviewer when it comes to Dune. I love it. I also have a lot of nostalgia for it because my husband and I read it together when we were dating!

So are there things that haven't aged well? Yes, for sure. Is it probably longer than it needed to be with uneven pacing? Sure, that is probably the case.

But do I particularly care?.... Not really. Honestly, it is in some ways a product of its time, but it's also such an iconic piece of science fiction that has had such major ripple effects. Reading the introduction in this new edition by Brian Herbert is fascinating and offers a window into the ideas and inspiration that led to this. It explores religion, philosophy, politics, and power. Taken together with Dune Messiah, it subverts the white savior trope in a way that actually made people angry back when it was published. (I'm begging you, don't stop here! At least read book 2 because there are seeds of his intentionality in book 1 that might not become clear until you read Dune Messiah) It may not be a perfect book, but it is a favorite for me and one that was in some ways ahead of its time.

Returning to this after a decade, I was surprised to find just how much of the book had really stuck with me in a way not many books do. This story and these characters have wormed their way in and I can't tell you how many times over the years I've thought of the litany for fear, for instance.

Anyway, I totally get that this won't be everyone's cup of tea, but I do think it's worth reading in a way that contextualizes the narrative Herbert was trying to tell. I'm still a fan!

I want to preface this review with the fact that sci-fi novels are not ones that I have any experience with. To be honest, I only decided to tackle this classic because of the film version having recently been released. Thought it was a great excuse to venture out of my literary comfort zone. So take this review with this in mind‚Ä¶

Going into this I had expectations of it being an epic adventure right up there with Lord of the Rings & Game of Thrones. Unfortunately, I was disappointed that it did not live up to either. The story itself wasn‚Äôt bad but the world building felt overly descriptive which distracted from the character building. I didn‚Äôt really feel like I got to really know any of the characters, which made me much less invested. Also, the story was not as action packed as I was hoping for‚Ä¶ actually a bit boring most of the way through. The ending was, also, pretty anti-climactic.

I listened to this on audio which was supposed to be multi narrated but ended up being a jumble of alternate voices mixed in with single narrating. This was extremely distracting and I think if the narration had stayed consistent throughout my enjoyment of the actual story may have been greater. Sadly, though the movie followed the book fairly accurately it wasn‚Äôt the ‚Äòepic‚Äô I was expecting either. So, overall, a pretty disappointing venture through all formats.

positive

Dune, the first novel in the Dune series, is (in my view) absolutely one of those life-changing reads. The novel is wildly imaginative, set in an unique dystopian future and populated with some of the most memorable characters. Dune takes world building on a whole new level but at the same time, it speaks of our present human society. It is a very relevant novel that raises numerous questions about religion, power, politics and identity. As many science fiction works I admire, Dune asks the question- What does it mean to be human? Some of its characters exhibit or acquire superhuman abilities, but even they are often trapped by the constraints of human society and their own social programming. It is fascinating to observe the conflict between the individual and the society in this book so rich in meaning and philosophical questions.

Dune is the best selling science fiction novel of all times- and deserving so. It wouldn't be far fetched to call it the best science fiction work of all times. When it comes to my personal reading experience, it is certainly one of the best novels I have read, science fiction or otherwise. Besides the absolute fascination the world building presented for me, I was (and still am) deeply fascinated with the individual characters. The psychological portrait of the characters is wonderful. The power structures in Dune are so well presented and an interesting area to study. There are so many fascinating societies within this dystopian world and they are all well presented in the characters themselves.

Dune is a world of the future but one where technology is taboo. Space travel exists, but planets are ruled by feudal houses - all of them united under the rule of a single king. The emperor with the noble houses may seem at the top, but it is the secret societies that hold much power- such as the 'witches'. What is so ingenious about Dune is that all of this world building just flows with the plot and the introduction of characters. The plot of the novel starts when a noble family Atreides has to leave a water planet they rule in an exchange for Dune (Rakis), a desert planet where 'spice' (a mysterious substance that elongates life and hold the Empire together) is produced by giant worms. The descriptions of planets are fantastic and many interesting questions are raised in terms of ecology, the effect we have on nature (and vice versa).

Duke Leto, the patriarch of a family is a deeply honorable man loved by many. His only heir is Paul, a boy described as unusually small for his age (but very clever and promising). Paul's mother Jessica, a Bene Gesserit pupil, is officially a concubine to Leto but is treated and loved as a wife. The reason why Duke Leto hadn't married her are strictly political. The love that exists in this relationship will have many interesting consequences. The product of that love is Paul- a boy who is under a watchful eyes of everyone. Early on, Paul learns some important lessons:

Dune starts of a story of a noble Atreides family and while it remains focused on this family (or the surviving members of it), with time Paul steps onto the scene. Indeed, it is Paul actions that change the course of history. Towards the end, there is no doubt who is the protagonist of this novel. As the young boy Paul risks his life to acquire supernatural abilities, we see him grown up into a man determined to do what it takes to save his family and claim what is his. However, what is the price Paul will have to pay?

‚ÄúGreatness is a transitory experience. It is never consistent. It depends in part upon the myth-making imagination of humankind. The person who experiences greatness must have a feeling for the myth he is in. He must reflect what is projected upon him. And he must have a strong sense of the sardonic. This is what uncouples him from belief in his own pretensions. The sardonic is all that permits him to move within himself. Without this quality, even occasional greatness will destroy a man.‚Äù


It is interesting how the characters that were created in this novel haunt all the sequels and sometimes even repeat again. Lady Jessica survives in the sisterhood, Idaho lives on in his gholas and Paul/Leto survive through all the numerous Atreides descendants. Although I honestly love all the sequels, I won't deny there is some magic at work in this first book in the series. It contains so much within itself. Highly recommended!

positive
~~~
While the ending felt abrupt and a bit unsatisfying, I very much enjoyed this classic of the sci-fi genre. Dune is considered the science fiction equivalent of Tolkien‚Äôs The Lord of the Rings, and that comparison makes total sense to me. The epic scope of Herbert‚Äôs debut novel, the highly unique setting, the near fathomless depth of the world building, the slightly stilted dialogue that lends the story a classic air, and the mysticism surrounding the central plot all reminded me of Tolkien‚Äôs world. Knowing how many books followed this one and dug further into the mythos Herbert introduced here further establishes that link. But what did for science fiction, how it helped propel the genre into the mainstream and show that it could appeal to literary readers, very much harkens to what Tolkien was able to do for the fantasy genre. Undoubtedly, the godfathers of genre fiction deserve our praise.

Going into this book knowing what it did for the genre and understanding that it‚Äôs a classic actually helped me enjoy it more than I otherwise would have. There was so much philosophy and so little deliverance of promised action sequences that I can see why plenty of others have been disappointed by . However, I try to read classics more leniently, with my focus on discovering what has enabled the book to not only stand the test of time, but how it influenced books that came after it. With this mindset, I enjoyed far more than I would have otherwise. While it is in my opinion far from a perfect novel, I can absolutely see why it‚Äôs important.

I found the characters interesting if often two-dimensional, but I found the world building and philosophy absolutely fascinating. I‚Äôm a sucker for religion in fiction, and delivered that in spades. Herbert obviously had a brilliant imagination, and I‚Äôm glad to have read this novel before new movie is released later this year. I‚Äôve never seen the original movie version, but after reading the book I‚Äôm incredibly excited to see this story play out on the big screen.

A buddy read with , , and !

‚Äî 4.5 ‚òÜ

with beautiful imagery and excellent prose, frank herbert creates desert planet, dune, and uses it to explore themes of human nature, morality, ecology, and systems of power. i really enjoyed this book. 'dune' perfectly embodies why i love fantasy/sci-fi so much: the political mind games, the genius creativity, the scope of discussions - it‚Äôs unbelievable how many topics frank herbert manages to touch on. i‚Äôm in awe.

primarily, this is a book critiquing white saviourism and empire. it gives agency to both the colonised environment and people by outlining the ways that white settlers exploit indigenous peoples, their faiths, and lands. i also loved the environmental commentary. herbert highlights the intersection between environmentalism and colonialism through ecological explorations. i loved that the setting was given just as much attention as the main characters are

i know this book can be controversial, and i think there's a lot to unpack with that. my stance is that the book does a fantastic job at depicting colonisation in its brutal & complex entirety. as frank herbert outlines: 'ecology is the science of understanding consequences' ‚Äî i think that perfectly summarises the heart of this novel & herbert's intention with this story

my only critiques are that some of the female characters deserved better (when is that not the case, though), and i have mixed feelings about the last third of the book. it felt messy to me (but i did audiobook parts of it, and that could be why)

on the whole, i think this is a mind-blowing sci-fi story that is unlike anything i‚Äôve ever read before. i would love to reread in the future and further pick all these aspects apart, but for now, i'm very excited to continue onto book 2!!

actually, i'm not overly sure what happened.

in fact, so much happened to where idk what actually happened.

what happened?

my brain, that's what happened. it can't make sense of anything complex.

this was a good book with good characters and good world building. that's why i'm giving this a good solid three!
I was just majorly confused for 75% of this book. And there was a stupid time jump, which i don't do well with reading time jumps (see Furyborn review), and that messed me up big time. Not to mention the audiobook cast. It's a great audiobook bc it has a full cast! but seriously they changed Paul's voice as he got older so there were like three different Paul's. that got me confused as well. Also the fact that this was written in the 60's makes it harder for me to read.

But overall it was a fun adventure when i knew what was going on. I wouldn't say it's a new favorite or that i loved it, but it was enjoyable and a fun buddyread w Gwen my twin <3

positive
~~~
3.5 Stars
I finally completed this epic sci fantasy novel! There is a lot of action and intrigue in this novel which certainly gave it a cinematic feel. (I'm very excited to watch the new 2021 movie, because I feel a story like this will translate to the big screen very well.)

Overall, I liked this story, but sadly never fell in love with it like other readers. It was incredibly tropey, and while I understand that it was the book created many of those tropes, I still struggle to get past how predictable the story felt. In a lot of ways this felt more like fantasy, with the space setting feeling like a backdrop. While I love to watch space fantasy, I rarely enjoy reading the subgenre. 

I am glad I read this one and would certainly be open to rereading it in the future. I hope it grows on me when I'm ready to experience it again. I know this book is so beloved and I really wanted it to become a new favourite.

of this wonder of literature, and I liked it as much as the first. And with more baggage I have enjoyed certain things even more.

This is the story of Paul Atreides, son of Duke Leto and Lady Jessica, rulers of a planet appointed by the Emperor to settle on the planet Arrakis instead of the Harkonnen, who ruled it until now. During the first Book (this novel is divided into 3 books) we will see the palatial plots of these vile Harkonnen to destroy the house of the Atreides, while in the remaining two we will see the story evolve. Inhabiting the planet are the Fremen, a people who live in a very tribal way, who call the planet Dune, and who will be a force to be reckoned with.

The book contains intrigues, adventures, philosophy, and some very well written characters. After the end there are a series of appendices that portray situations that occurred before the story, and a glossary with terminology of the Empire, which helps us to know more things than are mentioned in passing in the story.

If once you have reached the end you want to leave it like that, it is perfectly feasible, because everything is well tied. But if you want to continue, there are more novels to continue reading. A must-read for any science fiction fan.


--Important note, the review I had made in the original reading has been deleted.--

A gem of science fiction, one of the classics of the genre.
It has a very well developed story with a few previous brushstrokes of information, where the conspiracy and the palace plots carry a large part of the book. Some characters that I liked a lot, that also have, to a greater or lesser extent, a development.
I especially liked the glossary at the end, where they explain those words that you need to know but that give you a lot of information.
I have a rereading pending before the new film version, and I'm looking forward to it.

positive
~~~
re-read 2023 | the woman was too stunned to speak

_____

original review 2021 | phewww this review will be a HARD ONE to write. But I really want to talk about this book so here goes nothing.

Only time will tell i guess, but this might be a new all-time-favourite. I went into expecting to enjoy it, but I didn't expect to be so swept up in this world and its characters. It's been a long time since a book was able to consume me (and my life) this much and on so many levels. It took me almost two weeks to read it but it was worth every second.

There's so much to talk about that I don't quite know where to begin, and I'm sure I will forget a few things, but let's just go. One of the first things that surprised me was Frank Herbert's writing. I heard a lot of people say this was a very dense, dry and hard to get into novel. I would disagree on all of those aspects. I have seldom come across a novel so rich in beautiful, deep and meaningful quotes that really hit me. The themes Herbert discusses in Dune really resonated with me, which probably contributed to my enjoyment of his writing and how he used it to bring his messages across.

I'm very much team pro-epigraphs. I LOVED them. Not only for the beautiful quotes but also for increasing the tension of the book. Someone may argue that Princess Irulan spoils certain aspects of the story within these epigraphs and thus takes the tension out of the book. I would argue the opposite, but that's just my personal experience with the story.

Now, what I really love in SFF books is when the setting is almost its own character. And that is definitely the case in . The ecology of Arrakis and the environment these characters live in make up a huge part of the story and I loved it.

Though Frank Herbert does only offer a rather detached view of the characters, I did find myself rooting and caring for them a lot. I really enjoyed the mother-son-duo that was at the forefront throughout most of this book and the themes of parenthood and family that were therewith discussed. Lady Jessica is such an intriguing, strong and elegant character that I fell in love with pretty early on. I also really enjoyed Paul's character, although I did have some difficulties caring for him as a person later on in the book. That being said, I appreciate his character so much for what Frank Herbert does with it. I don't think I have ever followed a character quite like Paul before and I loved the themes that were explored through him, such as fear, destiny, manipulation and leadership (and what that actually entails). People call this book (or the movie) a white saviour story, and, to me, that discussion makes it pretty obvious who has actually read the source material and who hasn't. To me, this book is a critique of exactly that trope and opens up a LOT of discussion about culture, race and colonialism. But that's just my opinion.

That's not to say that this is a perfect book. I found the reliance on fatphobia to paint the picture of a detestable villain quite outdated and unnecessary. While I think that definitely stands the test of time in many aspects, the way women are treated in this universe could definitely be discussed further and I'm very curious to see these female characters we've come to love and care about develop even more in the upcoming instalments.

Overall, I think this book is more relevant than ever and can 100% be enjoyed by modern audiences. The topic of environmentalism alone is such a huge part of this story, next to various other thought-provoking themes that I greatly enjoyed. is so rich in social commentary, deep and fascinating concepts and human emotion. I have been looking for a book to really hit me for a while now, and I'm glad I found that book in Frank Herbert's . I can't wait to continue with the sequels and probably reread this story many, many times.

The world, the characters, the language, the politics, the‚Ä¶ everything! It can be overwhelming at times.

The story sort of lost me a little as Book II rolled in. I was really enjoying the political intrigue of the first part (this book is split up into 3 parts), and felt it killed off a few of the more promising players. That being said: we‚Äôre basically told from the beginning much of the things to come, and there were dozens more characters that came into play for me to invest in. Paul is a great protagonist, but I was all-in on Lady Jessica. She is, without doubt, one of the coolest characters I‚Äôve read in the genre (male, female or otherwise).

negative

What has mood to do with it? You fight when the necessity arises‚Äîno matter the mood! Mood's a thing for cattle or making love or playing the baliset. It's not for fighting.

My problems with Dune are not issues with speculation. My disbelief is maintained through healthy exercise. Much as I am opposed to terms like world-building I can empathize and imagine. Pondering alternatives is a heady philosophical endeavor. That said, I do not like the insistence on the epic in SF/F. What I want is a Month in The Country or a Stoner on some distant world. Maybe that is why Dhalgren and Embassytown hold such appeal. I read the first 300 pages in a rush. I noted that it was the cast of Hamlet involved in the Battle of Lepanto and deciding to stage a production of Othello. Along the way Ned Stark becomes a suicide bomber. Matters sort of went downhill from there. I didn‚Äôt pick up the book for a few weeks and then blew through the remaining 200 pages in two days. Lawrence of Arabia imbibes a magic elixir and becomes Harry Potter and the womenfolk just hope for the proper man, both in prophetic terms as well as totems of identity and fulfillment. Wooden dialogue muddied a clever use of stream-of-consciousness.

Alejandro Jodorowsky was an ultimate influence on my reading. His unrealized cinematic adaptation was a superior vision to what was committed to paper by Herbert. Here's to my pledge that the inchoate will never be terraformed.
